This allows developers to\\n25: // extend Kubernetes with their own APIs that behave like core Kubernetes APIs, complete\\n26: // with typed clients, authentication, authorization, and discovery.\\n27: //\\n28: // # Key Packages\\n29: //\\n30: // The `apiserver` library is composed of several key packages:\\n31: //\\n32: //   - `pkg/server`: This is the core of the library, providing the `GenericAPIServer`\\n33: //     and the main machinery for building the server.\\n34: //   - `pkg/admission`: This package contains the admission control framework. Developers\\n35: //     can use this to build custom admission plugins that can validate or mutate\\n36: //     requests to enforce custom policies. This is a common way to extend Kubernetes\\n37: //     behavior without adding a full API server.\\n38: //   - `pkg/authentication`: This package provides the framework for authenticating\\n39: //     requests.\\n40: //   - `pkg/authorization`: This package provides the framework for authorizing\\n41: //     requests.\\n42: //   - `pkg/endpoints`: This package contains the machinery for building the REST\\n43: //     endpoints for the API server.\\n44: //   - `pkg/registry`: This package provides the storage interface for the API server.\\n45: //\\n46: // # Instantiating a GenericAPIServer\\n47: //\\n48: // The `GenericAPIServer` struct is the heart of any extension server. It is responsible\\n49: // for assembling and running the HTTP serving stack. See the runnable example for a\\n50: // demonstration of how to instantiate a `GenericAPIServer`.\\n51: //\\n52: // # Building an Extension API Server (API Aggregation)\\n53: //\\n54: // The mechanism that enables extension API servers is API aggregation. The\\n55: // primary apiserver (typically the kube-apiserver) acts as a proxy, forwarding\\n56: // requests for a specific API group (e.g., /apis/myextension.io/v1) to a\\n57: // registered extension server. Admission plugins can be used to validate or mutate objects\\n67: // during write operations. The kube-apiserver uses admission plugins to provide\\n68: // a variety of core system capabilities.\\n69: //\\n70: // For most extension use cases dynamic admission control using policies\\n71: // (ValidatingAdmissionPolicies or MutatingAdmissionPolicies) or\\n72: // webhooks (ValidatingWebhookConfiguration and MutatingWebhookConfiguration) are the\\n73: // preferred way to extend admission control.\\n74: package apiserver\\n75: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/doc.go\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"3681fddd-bd37-4a65-b86f-7ae594ec71f5","uuid":"3855c17b-0f40-4e12-8710-b2377ba3050a","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"1: /*\\n2: Copyright 2021 The Kubernetes Authors.\\n3: \\n4: Licensed under the Apache License, Version 2.0 (the \\\"License\\\");\\n5: you may not use this file except in compliance with the License.\\n6: You may obtain a copy of the License at\\n7: \\n8:     http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\\n9: \\n10: Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\\n11: distributed under the License is distributed on an \\\"AS IS\\\" BASIS,\\n12: WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\\n13: See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\\n14: limitations under the License.\\n15: */\\n16: \\n17: // Package apiserver provides the machinery for building Kubernetes-style API servers.\\n18: //\\n19: // This library is the foundation for the Kubernetes API server (`kube-apiserver`),\\n20: // and is also the primary framework for developers building custom API servers to extend\\n21: // the Kubernetes API.\\n22: //\\n23: // An extension API server is a user-provided, standalone web server that registers itself\\n24: // with the main kube-apiserver to handle specific API groups. It is responsible\\n49: // for assembling and running the HTTP serving stack. See the runnable example for a\\n50: // demonstration of how to instantiate a `GenericAPIServer`.\\n51: //\\n52: // # Building an Extension API Server (API Aggregation)\\n53: //\\n54: // The mechanism that enables extension API servers is API aggregation. The\\n55: // primary apiserver (typically the kube-apiserver) acts as a proxy, forwarding\\n56: // requests for a specific API group (e.g., /apis/myextension.io/v1) to a\\n57: // registered extension server. The apiserver is configured using\\n58: // APIService objects.\\n59: //\\n60: // For most use cases, custom resources (CustomResourceDefinitions) are the\\n61: // preferred way to extend the Kubernetes API.\\n62: //\\n63: // # Building an Admission Plugin\\n64: //\\n65: // The `pkg/admission` package provides a way to add admission policies directly\\n66: // into an apiserver. Admission plugins can be used to validate or mutate objects\\n67: // during write operations. The kube-apiserver uses admission plugins to provide\\n68: // a variety of core system capabilities.\\n69: //\\n70: // For most extension use cases dynamic admission control using policies\\n71: // (ValidatingAdmissionPolicies or MutatingAdmissionPolicies) or\\n72: // webhooks (ValidatingWebhookConfiguration and MutatingWebhookConfiguration) are the\\n73: // preferred way to extend admission control.\\n74: package apiserver\\n75: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/doc.go\"}","structuredContent":{"content":"1: /*\n2: Copyright 2021 The Kubernetes Authors.\n3: \n4: Licensed under the Apache License, Version 2.0 (the \"License\");\n5: you may not use this file except in compliance with the License.\n6: You may obtain a copy of the License at\n7: \n8:     http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\n9: \n10: Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\n11: distributed under the License is distributed on an \"AS IS\" BASIS,\n12: WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\n13: See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\n14: limitations under the License.\n15: */\n16: \n17: // Package apiserver provides the machinery for building Kubernetes-style API servers.\n18: //\n19: // This library is the foundation for the Kubernetes API server (`kube-apiserver`),\n20: // and is also the primary framework for developers building custom API servers to extend\n21: // the Kubernetes API.\n22: //\n23: // An extension API server is a user-provided, standalone web server that registers itself\n24: // with the main kube-apiserver to handle specific API groups. This allows developers to\n25: // extend Kubernetes with their own APIs that behave like core Kubernetes APIs, complete\n26: // with typed clients, authentication, authorization, and discovery.\n27: //\n28: // # Key Packages\n29: //\n30: // The `apiserver` library is composed of several key packages:\n31: //\n32: //   - `pkg/server`: This is the core of the library, providing the `GenericAPIServer`\n33: //     and the main machinery for building the server.\n34: //   - `pkg/admission`: This package contains the admission control framework. Developers\n35: //     can use this to build custom admission plugins that can validate or mutate\n36: //     requests to enforce custom policies. This is a common way to extend Kubernetes\n37: //     behavior without adding a full API server.\n38: //   - `pkg/authentication`: This package provides the framework for authenticating\n39: //     requests.\n40: //   - `pkg/authorization`: This package provides the framework for authorizing\n41: //     requests.\n42: //   - `pkg/endpoints`: This package contains the machinery for building the REST\n43: //     endpoints for the API server.\n44: //   - `pkg/registry`: This package provides the storage interface for the API server.\n45: //\n46: // # Instantiating a GenericAPIServer\n47: //\n48: // The `GenericAPIServer` struct is the heart of any extension server. It is responsible\n49: // for assembling and running the HTTP serving stack. See the runnable example for a\n50: // demonstration of how to instantiate a `GenericAPIServer`.\n51: //\n52: // # Building an Extension API Server (API Aggregation)\n53: //\n54: // The mechanism that enables extension API servers is API aggregation. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01NHzkUQK5XMzpuZKirR1WxY","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"1: # apiserver Architecture\\n2: \\n3: ## 1. Server Composition\\n4: \\n5: The `kube-apiserver` binary is not one server, but a **server chain** of three distinct\\n6: `GenericAPIServer` instances: the core Kubernetes API, API extensions (CRDs), and the aggregation\\n7: layer.\\n8: \\n9: This composition is managed by a layered configuration system, starting with command-line\\n10: flags parsed by `options` structs (e.g., `RecommendedOptions` in `pkg/server/options`), which\\n11: populate a `Config` object that is then used to instantiate the `GenericAPIServer`\\n12: instances. The construction of this delegation chain can be found in the `CreateServerChain`\\n13: function in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go`.\\n14: \\n15: ```mermaid\\n16: graph TD\\n17:     subgraph Incoming Request\\n18:         direction LR\\n19:         A[User/Client] --> B{/apis/apps/v1/deployments};\\n20:     end\\n21: \\n22:     subgraph kube-apiserver process\\n23:         direction LR\\n24:         B --> C[Aggregator Server];\\n25:         C -- Not an APIService --> D[Kube API Server];\\n26:         D -- Handles Request --> E[REST Storage];\\n27:         C -- Is an APIService --> F[Proxy to Extension API Server];\\n28:         D -- Not a Core API --> G[API Extensions Server];\\n29:         G -- Handles CRD --> E;\\n30:     end\\n31: ```\\n32: \\n33: 1.  **Aggregator Server (`kube-aggregator`):**\\n34:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiregistration.k8s.io` API and acts as a reverse proxy for\\n35:         extension API servers. This functionality was designed to allow third-party APIs to be\\n36:         \\\"aggregated\\\" into the main Kubernetes API server seamlessly.\\n37:     *   **Mechanism:** It watches `APIService` objects. When a request arrives (e.g., for\\n38:         `/apis/mycompany.com/v1/myresources`), it checks if an `APIService` has \\\"claimed\\\"\\n39:         that path. If so, it uses a `ServiceResolver` to find the IP of the backing\\n40:         `Service` and proxies the request.\\n41:     *   **Use Case:** This pattern is for programmatic, high-control extensions that require\\n42:         custom business logic (e.g., non-CRUD subresources like `/logs`) or alternative\\n43:         storage backends.\\n44:     *   **Delegation:** If no `APIService` matches, it delegates the request to the next\\n45:         server in the chain.\\n46: \\n47: 2.  **Kube API Server (Core):**\\n48:     *   **Purpose:** Serves all the built-in Kubernetes APIs (`core/v1`, `apps/v1`, etc.).\\n49:     *   **Mechanism:** This is the main server, configured with all the core REST storage\\n50:         strategies.\\n51:     *   **Delegation:** If a request is for a path that is not a core API (e.g., for a CRD),\\n52:         it delegates the request to the next server in the chain.\\n53: \\n54: 3.  **API Extensions Server (`apiextensions-apiserver`):**\\n55:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiextensions.k8s.io` API, which manages\\n56:         `CustomResourceDefinition` objects (CRDs). The evolution of CRDs from a simple extension\\n57:         mechanism to a feature-rich system with validation, versioning, and defaulting is\\n58:         documented in a series of KEPs, starting with the graduation to GA in Kubernetes v1.16.\\n59:     *   **Mechanism:** When a CRD is created, this server dynamically creates and installs a\\n60:         new REST storage handler for the new resource, making it immediately available.\\n61:     *   **Use Case:** CRDs are the most common extension pattern, offering a declarative,\\n62:         schema-based way to define new resource types that are stored in etcd and require no\\n63:         custom API server code.\\n64:     *   **Delegation:** It is the end of the chain. If it cannot handle a request, a `404 Not\\n65:         Found` is returned.\\n66: \\n67: ## 2. Handler Chain\\n68: \\n69: Every request flows through a standard chain of HTTP handlers (filters). The request body\\n70: is not deserialized until it has passed authentication and authorization. The default handler\\n71: chain is constructed by the `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` function in\\n72: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`.\\n73: \\n74: ```mermaid\\n75: sequenceDiagram\\n76:     participant Client\\n77:     participant Handler Chain\\n78:     participant Authentication\\n79:     participant Authorization\\n80:     participant Priority and Fairness\\n81:     participant Admission Control\\n82:     participant REST Endpoint\\n83: \\n84:     Client->>Handler Chain: Request\\n85:     Handler Chain->>Authentication: Authenticate\\n86:     Authentication-->>Handler Chain: User Info\\n87:     Handler Chain->>Authorization: Authorize\\n88:     Authorization-->>Handler Chain: Allowed/Denied\\n89:     Handler Chain->>Priority and Fairness: Classify & Queue\\n90:     Priority and Fairness-->>Handler Chain: Proceed\\n91:     Handler Chain->>Admission Control: Mutate & Validate\\n92:     Admission Control-->>Handler Chain: Object OK\\n93:     Handler Chain->>REST Endpoint: Handle\\n94:     REST Endpoint-->>Handler Chain: Response\\n95:     Handler Chain-->>Client: Response\\n96: ```\\n97: \\n98: The handler chain consists of the following stages:\\n99: \\n100: 1.  **Authentication (`pkg/authentication`):** This filter identifies the user. The system is\\n101:     pluggable and composed of multiple authenticators (e.g., client certs, bearer tokens, OIDC).\\n102:     The identity of the user is determined by the first authenticator in the chain that successfully\\n103:     identifies the user.\\n104: 2.  **Authorization (`pkg/authorization`):** This filter checks if the user is permitted to\\n105:     perform the action. This system is also pluggable and composed of multiple authorizers\\n106:     (e.g., RBAC, Node, Webhook). Each authorizer may respond with either allow, deny, or no opinion.\\n107:     If the response is no opinion, the request is passed to the next authorizer in the chain.\\n108: 3.  **Priority and Fairness (`pkg/util/flowcontrol`):** This subsystem manages request\\n109:     concurrency, classifying requests into `FlowSchema`s and `PriorityLevel`s to prevent\\n110:     overload. This feature was introduced to prevent high traffic from overwhelming the API\\n111:     server and to ensure that critical cluster operations are not starved.\\n112: 4.  **Admission Control (`pkg/admission`):** This is the primary mechanism for policy\\n113:     enforcement. It is only at this stage that the request body is deserialized into an\\n114:     object. It is a chain of plugins that can mutate or validate an object. The built-in Pod\\n115:     Security admission controller is a key example of this, enforcing Pod Security Standards\\n116:     at the namespace level.\\n117: 5.  **REST Endpoint Handling (`pkg/endpoints`):** The request is finally dispatched to the\\n118:     appropriate REST handler, which is installed by the `APIInstaller`.\\n119: \\n120: ## 3. API Group Registration\\n121: \\n122: The high-level steps for introducing an API are:\\n123: \\n124: 1.  **Define Types:** Create or modify the Go structs in the `types.go` file for the API group.\\n125: 2.  **Generate Code:** Use the code generators provided by the Kubernetes project to create the required\\n126:     boilerplate methods for deep-copy, conversion, and defaulting.\\n127: 3.  **Implement the `Strategy`:** Write the custom business logic and validation for the\\n128:     resource in its `Strategy` object.\\n129: 4.  **Register and Install:** Create the `APIGroupInfo` struct, bundling the `Scheme` and the\\n130:     `Strategy`-configured storage, and pass it to the `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup`\\n131:     method.\\n132: \\n133: ### The API Group Registry\\n134: \\n135: The `runtime.Scheme` acts as a central registry for an API group's type information. A single\\n136: `Scheme` object is created for each API group and is responsible for the following key\\n137: capabilities:\\n138: \\n139: *   **Type Registration and Mapping:** The `Scheme`'s primary role is to map a GroupVersionKind\\n140:     (GVK) to its corresponding Go type and back. This process also relies on the `deepcopy-gen`\\n141:     tool to create `DeepCopy()` methods for each type, which is critical for ensuring that\\n142:     objects returned from caches are never modified directly.\\n143: \\n144: *   **API Conversion:** The `Scheme` stores the conversion functions that translate objects\\n145:     between different API versions. These functions are typically generated by the\\n146:     `conversion-gen` tool and enable the **hub-and-spoke** model.\\n147: \\n148: *   **Defaulting:** The `Scheme` registers defaulting functions that populate optional fields in\\n149:     an object. These are usually generated by the `defaulter-gen` tool.\\n150: \\n151: *   **Declarative Validation:** The `Scheme` can store and execute code-generated validation\\n152:     functions, providing a baseline level of validation. This is distinct from the primary,\\n153:     handwritten business logic validation, which is handled by the `Strategy` object.\\n154: \\n155: ### The `APIGroupInfo` Struct and `Strategy` Object\\n156: \\n157: With a populated `Scheme`, the API group is registered with the `GenericAPIServer` by bundling\\n158: the `Scheme` with the storage backend and versioning information into an `APIGroupInfo` struct.\\n159: \\n160: ```mermaid\\n161: graph TD\\n162:     subgraph Server Configuration\\n163:         A[APIGroupInfo for apps v1];\\n164:         A --> B{Scheme: Knows Deployment v1};\\n165:         A --> C{Storage: deployments RESTStorage};\\n166:         A --> D{Version Priority: v1, v1beta1};\\n167:     end\\n168: \\n169:     subgraph RESTStorage Implementation\\n170:         C --> E[genericregistry.Store];\\n171:         E --> F[etcd client];\\n172:         E --> G[Deployment Strategy];\\n173:     end\\n174: \\n175:     subgraph Server Runtime\\n176:         H[GenericAPIServer] -- InstallAPIGroup --> I[APIInstaller];\\n177:         I -- Uses --> A;\\n178:         I --> J{Register /apis/apps/v1/deployments};\\n179:         J --> K[HTTP Handler];\\n180:         K -- On Request --> C;\\n181:     end\\n182: ```\\n183: \\n184: The registration process follows these steps:\\n185: \\n186: 1.  **`APIGroupInfo` Construction:** For each API group, an `APIGroupInfo` struct is created,\\n187:     which contains the populated `Scheme`, a map of resources to their storage\\n188:     implementations, and an ordered list of **Version Priority**.\\n189: \\n190: 2.  **REST Storage Instantiation:** For each resource, a `genericregistry.Store` is created. It\\n191:     is configured with a resource-specific `Strategy` object that contains the core business\\n192:     logic (e.g., handwritten validation).\\n193: \\n194: 3.  **API Group Installation:** The `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup` method takes the\\n195:     `APIGroupInfo` and uses an `APIInstaller` to expose the resources as HTTP endpoints.\\n196: \\n197: ## 4. Watch Cache\\n198: \\n199: To handle the high volume of watch requests from controllers without overwhelming etcd, the\\n200: apiserver uses a **watch cache**. The implementation can be found in\\n201: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/`.\\n202: \\n203: *   **Initialization:** The cacher first performs a `LIST` to get the current state of all\\n204:     objects and a `ResourceVersion` for that point-in-time. It then starts a `WATCH` from\\n205:     that version to ensure a consistent stream of events.\\n206: *   **Serving from Cache:** Most list and watch requests are served from this in-memory cache, which\\n207:     dramatically reduces the load on etcd. Consistent reads are also served from the\\n208:     cache. This is achieved by first fetching the revision number of the latest write from\\n209:     etcd. The server then ensures the cache is at least that recent—waiting for it to\\n210:     refresh if necessary—before serving the request.\\n211: *   **Fallback to Storage:** If a client request cannot be served from the\\n212:     cache's buffer, the request \\\"falls through\\\" to the underlying etcd storage.\\n213: *   **Bookmarks:** The cacher uses bookmark events to track the latest `ResourceVersion` for\\n214:     unchanged objects. This prevents the cache's `ResourceVersion` from becoming too old,\\n215:     which avoids the need for expensive relist operations from etcd when the objects have\\n216:     not been modified.\\n217: \\n218: ## 5. Conflict Resolution\\n219: \\n220: *   **Optimistic Concurrency via `resourceVersion`:** Clients are expected to perform updates using a\\n221:     read-modify-write workflow. The apiserver uses the `resourceVersion` field of every\\n222:     object to enforce optimistic concurrency. This `resourceVersion` is not an arbitrary number;\\n223:     it maps directly to etcd's globally consistent `mod_revision`. When a client submits an\\n224:     update (`PUT` or `PATCH`), it must provide the `resourceVersion` of the object it based its\\n225:     modifications on. If the `resourceVersion` on the server does not match the current\\n226:     `mod_revision` in etcd, the server rejects the request with a `409 Conflict` error. This\\n227:     forces the client to re-read the object, resolve the conflict, and resubmit with the new\\n228:     `resourceVersion`.\\n229: *   **Server-Side Apply:** A declarative, \\\"intent-based\\\" patch. The server maintains a\\n230:     `managedFields` section in the object's metadata to track which \\\"manager\\\" (e.g., a\\n231:     controller) owns each field. This allows multiple actors to manage different parts of the\\n232:     same object without overwriting each other's changes.\\n233: \\n234: ## 6. Discovery and OpenAPI\\n235: \\n236: Apiservers serve the `/apis` discovery endpoints and the `/openapi/v2` and `/openapi/v3`\\n237: specifications. The generation of the OpenAPI specification is a multi-stage process.\\n238: \\n239: *   **`openapi-gen`**: This tool reflects on Go structs, reads godoc comments, and looks at\\n240:     validation struct tags to generate a map of all API definitions.\\n241: *   **`zz_generated.openapi.go`**: The output is a large Go file containing a\\n242:     `GetOpenAPIDefinitions` function.\\n243: *   **Runtime**: The `GenericAPIServer` calls this generated function to build the final OpenAPI\\n244:     JSON spec that it serves to clients.\\n245: \\n246: ## 7. Security & Observability\\n247: \\n248: *   **Audit (`pkg/audit`):** The apiserver has a policy-driven event logging pipeline. The audit\\n249:     policy controls what is logged and at which stage of a request.\\n250: *   **Security:**\\n251:     *   **mTLS:** The primary authentication mechanism for system components.\\n252:     *   **Service Account Token Issuance:** The `kube-apiserver` acts as an OIDC provider,\\n253:         issuing and validating JWTs for `ServiceAccount`s.\\n254: \\n255: ## 8. Streaming Protocols\\n256: \\n257: *   **Websockets:** The apiserver uses websockets to upgrade HTTP\\n258:     connections for interactive, streaming protocols like `exec`, `attach`, and\\n259:     `port-forward`. The `UpgradeAwareProxyHandler` manages this process.\\n260: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"3681fddd-bd37-4a65-b86f-7ae594ec71f5","uuid":"2115308b-5b44-4bcc-b21d-41db1adf5e62","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"1: # apiserver Architecture\\n2: \\n3: ## 1. The default handler\\n71: chain is constructed by the `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` function in\\n72: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`.\\n73: \\n74: ```mermaid\\n75: sequenceDiagram\\n76:     participant Client\\n77:     participant Handler Chain\\n78:     participant Authentication\\n79:     participant Authorization\\n80:     participant Priority and Fairness\\n81:     participant Admission Control\\n82:     participant REST Endpoint\\n83: \\n84:     Client->>Handler Chain: Request\\n85:     Handler Chain->>Authentication: Authenticate\\n86:     Authentication-->>Handler Chain: User Info\\n87:     Handler Chain->>Authorization: Authorize\\n88:     Authorization-->>Handler Chain: Allowed/Denied\\n89:     Handler Chain->>Priority and Fairness: Classify & Queue\\n90:     Priority and Fairness-->>Handler Chain: Proceed\\n91:     Handler Chain->>Admission Control: Mutate & Validate\\n92:     Admission Control-->>Handler Chain: Object OK\\n93:     Handler Chain->>REST Endpoint: Handle\\n94:     REST Endpoint-->>Handler Chain: Response\\n95:     Handler Chain-->>Client: Response\\n96: ```\\n97: \\n98: The handler chain consists of the following stages:\\n99: \\n100: 1.  **Authentication (`pkg/authentication`):** This filter identifies the user. The system is\\n101:     pluggable and composed of multiple authenticators (e.g., client certs, bearer tokens, OIDC).\\n102:     The identity of the user is determined by the first authenticator in the chain that successfully\\n103:     identifies the user.\\n104: 2.  **Authorization (`pkg/authorization`):** This filter checks if the user is permitted to\\n105:     perform the action. This system is also pluggable and composed of multiple authorizers\\n106:     (e.g., RBAC, Node, Webhook). Each authorizer may respond with either allow, deny, or no opinion.\\n107:     If the response is no opinion, the request is passed to the next authorizer in the chain.\\n108: 3.  **Priority and Fairness (`pkg/util/flowcontrol`):** This subsystem manages request\\n109:     concurrency, classifying requests into `FlowSchema`s and `PriorityLevel`s to prevent\\n110:     overload. This feature was introduced to prevent high traffic from overwhelming the API\\n111:     server and to ensure that critical cluster operations are not starved.\\n112: 4.  **Admission Control (`pkg/admission`):** This is the primary mechanism for policy\\n113:     enforcement. It is only at this stage that the request body is deserialized into an\\n114:     object. It is a chain of plugins that can mutate or validate an object. The built-in Pod\\n115:     Security admission controller is a key example of this, enforcing Pod Security Standards\\n116:     at the namespace level.\\n117: 5.  **REST Endpoint Handling (`pkg/endpoints`):** The request is finally dispatched to the\\n118:     appropriate REST handler, which is installed by the `APIInstaller`.\\n119: \\n120: ## 3. API Group Registration\\n121: \\n122: The high-level steps for introducing an API are:\\n123: \\n124: 1.  **Define Types:** Create or modify the Go structs in the `types.go` file for the API group.\\n125: 2.  **Generate Code:** Use the code generators provided by the Kubernetes project to create the required\\n126:     boilerplate methods for deep-copy, conversion, and defaulting.\\n127: 3.  **Implement the `Strategy`:** Write the custom business logic and validation for the\\n128:     resource in its `Strategy` object.\\n129: 4.  **Register and Install:** Create the `APIGroupInfo` struct, bundling the `Scheme` and the\\n130:     `Strategy`-configured storage, and pass it to the `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup`\\n131:     method.\\n132: \\n133: ### The API Group Registry\\n134: \\n135: The `runtime.Scheme` acts as a central registry for an API group's type information. A single\\n136: `Scheme` object is created for each API group and is responsible for the following key\\n137: capabilities:\\n138: \\n139: *   **Type Registration and Mapping:** The `Scheme`'s primary role is to map a GroupVersionKind\\n140:     (GVK) to its corresponding Go type and back. This process also relies on the `deepcopy-gen`\\n141:     tool to create `DeepCopy()` methods for each type, which is critical for ensuring that\\n142:     objects returned from caches are never modified directly.\\n143: \\n144: *   **API Conversion:** The `Scheme` stores the conversion functions that translate objects\\n145:     between different API versions. These functions are typically generated by the\\n146:     `conversion-gen` tool and enable the **hub-and-spoke** model.\\n147: \\n148: *   **Defaulting:** The `Scheme` registers defaulting functions that populate optional fields in\\n149:     an object. These are usually generated by the `defaulter-gen` tool.\\n150: \\n151: *   **Declarative Validation:** The `Scheme` can store and execute code-generated validation\\n152:     functions, providing a baseline level of validation. This is distinct from the primary,\\n153:     handwritten business logic validation, which is handled by the `Strategy` object.\\n154: \\n155: ### The `APIGroupInfo` Struct and `Strategy` Object\\n156: \\n157: With a populated `Scheme`, the API group is registered with the `GenericAPIServer` by bundling\\n158: the `Scheme` with the storage backend and versioning information into an `APIGroupInfo` struct.\\n159: \\n160: ```mermaid\\n161: graph TD\\n162:     subgraph Server Configuration\\n163:         A[APIGroupInfo for apps v1];\\n164:         A --> B{Scheme: Knows Deployment v1};\\n165:         A --> C{Storage: deployments RESTStorage};\\n166:         A --> D{Version Priority: v1, v1beta1};\\n167:     end\\n168: \\n169:     subgraph RESTStorage Implementation\\n170:         C --> E[genericregistry.Store];\\n171:         E --> F[etcd client];\\n172:         E --> G[Deployment Strategy];\\n173:     end\\n174: \\n175:     subgraph Server Runtime\\n176:         H[GenericAPIServer] -- InstallAPIGroup --> I[APIInstaller];\\n177:         I -- Uses --> A;\\n178:         I --> J{Register /apis/apps/v1/deployments};\\n179:         J --> K[HTTP Handler];\\n180:         K -- On Request --> C;\\n181:     end\\n182: ```\\n183: \\n184: The registration process follows these steps:\\n185: \\n186: 1.  **`APIGroupInfo` Construction:** For each API group, an `APIGroupInfo` struct is created,\\n187:     which contains the populated `Scheme`, a map of resources to their storage\\n188:     implementations, and an ordered list of **Version Priority**.\\n189: \\n190: 2.  **REST Storage Instantiation:** For each resource, a `genericregistry.Store` is created. It\\n191:     is configured with a resource-specific `Strategy` object that contains the core business\\n192:     logic (e.g., handwritten validation).\\n193: \\n194: 3.  **API Group Installation:** The `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup` method takes the\\n195:     `APIGroupInfo` and uses an `APIInstaller` to expose the resources as HTTP endpoints.\\n196: \\n197: ## 4. Watch Cache\\n198: \\n199: To handle the high volume of watch requests from controllers without overwhelming etcd, the\\n200: apiserver uses a **watch cache**. The implementation can be found in\\n201: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/`.\\n202: \\n203: *   **Initialization:** The cacher first performs a `LIST` to get the current state of all\\n204:     objects and a `ResourceVersion` for that point-in-time. It then starts a `WATCH` from\\n205:     that version to ensure a consistent stream of events.\\n206: *   **Serving from Cache:** Most list and watch requests are served from this in-memory cache, which\\n207:     dramatically reduces the load on etcd. Consistent reads are also served from the\\n208:     cache. This is achieved by first fetching the revision number of the latest write from\\n209:     etcd. The server then ensures the cache is at least that recent—waiting for it to\\n210:     refresh if necessary—before serving the request.\\n211: *   **Fallback to Storage:** If a client request cannot be served from the\\n212:     cache's buffer, the request \\\"falls through\\\" to the underlying etcd storage.\\n213: *   **Bookmarks:** The cacher uses bookmark events to track the latest `ResourceVersion` for\\n214:     unchanged objects. This prevents the cache's `ResourceVersion` from becoming too old,\\n215:     which avoids the need for expensive relist operations from etcd when the objects have\\n216:     not been modified.\\n217: \\n218: ## 5. Conflict Resolution\\n219: \\n220: *   **Optimistic Concurrency via `resourceVersion`:** Clients are expected to perform updates using a\\n221:     read-modify-write workflow. The apiserver uses the `resourceVersion` field of every\\n222:     object to enforce optimistic concurrency. This `resourceVersion` is not an arbitrary number;\\n223:     it maps directly to etcd's globally consistent `mod_revision`. When a client submits an\\n224:     update (`PUT` or `PATCH`), it must provide the `resourceVersion` of the object it based its\\n225:     modifications on. If the `resourceVersion` on the server does not match the current\\n226:     `mod_revision` in etcd, the server rejects the request with a `409 Conflict` error. This\\n227:     forces the client to re-read the object, resolve the conflict, and resubmit with the new\\n228:     `resourceVersion`.\\n229: *   **Server-Side Apply:** A declarative, \\\"intent-based\\\" patch. The server maintains a\\n230:     `managedFields` section in the object's metadata to track which \\\"manager\\\" (e.g., a\\n231:     controller) owns each field. This allows multiple actors to manage different parts of the\\n232:     same object without overwriting each other's changes.\\n233: \\n234: ## 6. Discovery and OpenAPI\\n235: \\n236: Apiservers serve the `/apis` discovery endpoints and the `/openapi/v2` and `/openapi/v3`\\n237: specifications. The generation of the OpenAPI specification is a multi-stage process.\\n238: \\n239: *   **`openapi-gen`**: This tool reflects on Go structs, reads godoc comments, and looks at\\n240:     validation struct tags to generate a map of all API definitions.\\n241: *   **`zz_generated.openapi.go`**: The output is a large Go file containing a\\n242:     `GetOpenAPIDefinitions` function.\\n243: *   **Runtime**: The `GenericAPIServer` calls this generated function to build the final OpenAPI\\n244:     JSON spec that it serves to clients.\\n245: \\n246: ## 7. Security & Observability\\n247: \\n248: *   **Audit (`pkg/audit`):** The apiserver has a policy-driven event logging pipeline. The audit\\n249:     policy controls what is logged and at which stage of a request.\\n250: *   **Security:**\\n251:     *   **mTLS:** The primary authentication mechanism for system components.\\n252:     *   **Service Account Token Issuance:** The `kube-apiserver` acts as an OIDC provider,\\n253:         issuing and validating JWTs for `ServiceAccount`s.\\n254: \\n255: ## 8. Streaming Protocols\\n256: \\n257: *   **Websockets:** The apiserver uses websockets to upgrade HTTP\\n258:     connections for interactive, streaming protocols like `exec`, `attach`, and\\n259:     `port-forward`. The `UpgradeAwareProxyHandler` manages this process.\\n260: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}","structuredContent":{"content":"1: # apiserver Architecture\n2: \n3: ## 1. Server Composition\n4: \n5: The `kube-apiserver` binary is not one server, but a **server chain** of three distinct\n6: `GenericAPIServer` instances: the core Kubernetes API, API extensions (CRDs), and the aggregation\n7: layer.\n8: \n9: This composition is managed by a layered configuration system, starting with command-line\n10: flags parsed by `options` structs (e.g., `RecommendedOptions` in `pkg/server/options`), which\n11: populate a `Config` object that is then used to instantiate the `GenericAPIServer`\n12: instances. The construction of this delegation chain can be found in the `CreateServerChain`\n13: function in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go`.\n14: \n15: ```mermaid\n16: graph TD\n17:     subgraph Incoming Request\n18:         direction LR\n19:         A[User/Client] --> B{/apis/apps/v1/deployments};\n20:     end\n21: \n22:     subgraph kube-apiserver process\n23:         direction LR\n24:         B --> C[Aggregator Server];\n25:         C -- Not an APIService --> D[Kube API Server];\n26:         D -- Handles Request --> E[REST Storage];\n27:         C -- Is an APIService --> F[Proxy to Extension API Server];\n28:         D -- Not a Core API --> G[API Extensions Server];\n29:         G -- Handles CRD --> E;\n30:     end\n31: ```\n32: \n33: 1.  **Aggregator Server (`kube-aggregator`):**\n34:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiregistration.k8s.io` API and acts as a reverse proxy for\n35:         extension API servers. This functionality was designed to allow third-party APIs to be\n36:         \"aggregated\" into the main Kubernetes API server seamlessly.\n37:     *   **Mechanism:** It watches `APIService` objects. When a request arrives (e.g., for\n38:         `/apis/mycompany.com/v1/myresources`), it checks if an `APIService` has \"claimed\"\n39:         that path. If so, it uses a `ServiceResolver` to find the IP of the backing\n40:         `Service` and proxies the request.\n41:     *   **Use Case:** This pattern is for programmatic, high-control extensions that require\n42:         custom business logic (e.g., non-CRUD subresources like `/logs`) or alternative\n43:         storage backends.\n44:     *   **Delegation:** If no `APIService` matches, it delegates the request to the next\n45:         server in the chain.\n46: \n47: 2.  **Kube API Server (Core):**\n48:     *   **Purpose:** Serves all the built-in Kubernetes APIs (`core/v1`, `apps/v1`, etc.).\n49:     *   **Mechanism:** This is the main server, configured with all the core REST storage\n50:         strategies.\n51:     *   **Delegation:** If a request is for a path that is not a core API (e.g., for a CRD),\n52:         it delegates the request to the next server in the chain.\n53: \n54: 3.  **API Extensions Server (`apiextensions-apiserver`):**\n55:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiextensions.k8s.io` API, which manages\n56:         `CustomResourceDefinition` objects (CRDs). The evolution of CRDs from a simple extension\n57:         mechanism to a feature-rich system with validation, versioning, and defaulting is\n58:         documented in a series of KEPs, starting with the graduation to GA in Kubernetes v1.16.\n59:     *   **Mechanism:** When a CRD is created, this server dynamically creates and installs a\n60:         new REST storage handler for the new resource, making it immediately available.\n61:     *   **Use Case:** CRDs are the most common extension pattern, offering a declarative,\n62:         schema-based way to define new resource types that are stored in etcd and require no\n63:         custom API server code.\n64:     *   **Delegation:** It is the end of the chain. If it cannot handle a request, a `404 Not\n65:         Found` is returned.\n66: \n67: ## 2. Handler Chain\n68: \n69: Every request flows through a standard chain of HTTP handlers (filters). The request body\n70: is not deserialized until it has passed authentication and authorization. The default handler\n71: chain is constructed by the `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` function in\n72: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`.\n73: \n74: ```mermaid\n75: sequenceDiagram\n76:     participant Client\n77:     participant Handler Chain\n78:     participant Authentication\n79:     participant Authorization\n80:     participant Priority and Fairness\n81:     participant Admission Control\n82:     participant REST Endpoint\n83: \n84:     Client->>Handler Chain: Request\n85:     Handler Chain->>Authentication: Authenticate\n86:     Authentication-->>Handler Chain: User Info\n87:     Handler Chain->>Authorization: Authorize\n88:     Authorization-->>Handler Chain: Allowed/Denied\n89:     Handler Chain->>Priority and Fairness: Classify & Queue\n90:     Priority and Fairness-->>Handler Chain: Proceed\n91:     Handler Chain->>Admission Control: Mutate & Validate\n92:     Admission Control-->>Handler Chain: Object OK\n93:     Handler Chain->>REST Endpoint: Handle\n94:     REST Endpoint-->>Handler Chain: Response\n95:     Handler Chain-->>Client: Response\n96: ```\n97: \n98: The handler chain consists of the following stages:\n99: \n100: 1.  **Authentication (`pkg/authentication`):** This filter identifies the user. The system is\n101:     pluggable and composed of multiple authenticators (e.g., client certs, bearer tokens, OIDC).\n102:     The identity of the user is determined by the first authenticator in the chain that successfully\n103:     identifies the user.\n104: 2.  **Authorization (`pkg/authorization`):** This filter checks if the user is permitted to\n105:     perform the action. Each authorizer may respond with either allow, deny, or no opinion.\n107:     If the response is no opinion, the request is passed to the next authorizer in the chain.\n108: 3.  **Priority and Fairness (`pkg/util/flowcontrol`):** This subsystem manages request\n109:     concurrency, classifying requests into `FlowSchema`s and `PriorityLevel`s to prevent\n110:     overload. This feature was introduced to prevent high traffic from overwhelming the API\n111:     server and to ensure that critical cluster operations are not starved.\n112: 4.  **Admission Control (`pkg/admission`):** This is the primary mechanism for policy\n113:     enforcement. It is only at this stage that the request body is deserialized into an\n114:     object. It is a chain of plugins that can mutate or validate an object. The built-in Pod\n115:     Security admission controller is a key example of this, enforcing Pod Security Standards\n116:     at the namespace level.\n117: 5.  **REST Endpoint Handling (`pkg/endpoints`):** The request is finally dispatched to the\n118:     appropriate REST handler, which is installed by the `APIInstaller`.\n119: \n120: ## 3. It abstracts the actual underlying objects to prevent coupling with concrete\\n75: // database and to improve testability.\\n76: type ResponseMeta struct {\\n77: \\t// TTL is the time to live of the node that contained the returned object. {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-haiku-4-5-20251001","id":"msg_01WVXNjkJnoygLDkvDDBPTax","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"tool_use","id":"toolu_01SRd8xLbV6emF5HdnqGdcgw","name":"Write","input":{"file_path":"/workspace/documentation.md","content":"# Kubernetes API Server Library: Architecture and Extension Guide\n\n## Executive Summary\n\nThe Kubernetes `apiserver` library (`staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver`) is a reusable framework for building Kubernetes-compatible API servers. It abstracts the common machinery required for REST API serving, request filtering, admission control, storage abstraction, and watch caching. The library is the foundation of the official kube-apiserver and is designed to enable custom API servers to provide first-class Kubernetes experiences without reimplementing core infrastructure.\n\n---\n\n## 1. Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies\n\n### Purpose\n\nThe apiserver library solves the problem of building production-grade REST APIs that behave consistently with Kubernetes. Rather than requiring each API server to implement authentication, authorization, admission control, audit logging, storage coordination, and conflict resolution independently, the library provides:\n\n- **Unified HTTP request handling pipeline** (authentication → authorization → admission → storage)\n- **Abstract storage backend** enabling multiple data store implementations (primarily etcd)\n- **Request rate limiting and concurrency management** via Priority and Fairness subsystem\n- **Watch caching layer** reducing backend storage load\n- **Pluggable extension points** for custom business logic via admission control and storage strategies\n\n### Subsystem Boundaries\n\nThe apiserver library **owns**:\n- HTTP handler chain construction and filter ordering\n- Request parsing, validation, and serialization/deserialization\n- Authentication and authorization verification\n- Admission control enforcement (validation and mutation)\n- REST endpoint registration and discovery\n- Watch request buffering and event streaming\n- Resource version (optimistic concurrency control)\n- Server lifecycle hooks (pre-start, post-start, pre-shutdown)\n\nThe apiserver library **does NOT own**:\n- Storage backend implementation (delegates via interface)\n- Custom controller logic or watch handlers\n- Specific Kubernetes resource types (apps/v1 Deployment, core/v1 Pod, etc.)\n- TLS certificate management (provided separately)\n- Metrics collection (integrates with external Prometheus systems)\n\n### Upstream Dependencies\n\n- **`k8s.io/apimachinery`**: Core types (`metav1`, `runtime.Scheme`, `runtime.Object`), serialization, and conversion machinery\n- **`k8s.io/component-base`**: Common component utilities (feature gates, logging, metrics)\n- **`github.com/emicklei/go-restful`**: HTTP web service framework for API endpoint registration\n- **etcd gRPC client**: Backing storage for persisted resources (optional; other backends possible)\n\n### Downstream Consumers\n\n- **`kube-apiserver`** (cmd/kube-apiserver): Official Kubernetes API server\n- **`kube-aggregator`** (staging/src/k8s.io/kube-aggregator): API aggregation and delegation layer\n- **`apiextensions-apiserver`** (staging/src/k8s.io/apiextensions-apiserver): CustomResourceDefinition handler\n- **Extension API servers** (sample-apiserver, custom operators): Custom domain APIs\n\n---\n\n## 2. Key Components and Responsibility Split\n\n### 2.1 GenericAPIServer (`pkg/server/genericapiserver.go`)\n\n**Responsibility**: Central orchestrator for request handling and API group lifecycle.\n\n**Key Role**: The `GenericAPIServer` struct is the root object responsible for:\n- Managing the HTTP handler chain (via `APIServerHandler`)\n- Installing API groups and their REST endpoints\n- Coordinating storage initialization and cleanup\n- Running PostStartHooks and PreShutdownHooks\n- Maintaining discovery and OpenAPI specifications\n- Graceful shutdown with request draining\n\n**Example construction** (from the source):\n```go\ntype GenericAPIServer struct {\n    Handler *APIServerHandler              // HTTP handler delegation\n    admissionControl admission.Interface   // Admission plugins\n    SecureServingInfo *SecureServingInfo   // TLS configuration\n    delegationTarget DelegationTarget       // Next server in chain\n    postStartHooks map[string]postStartHookEntry\n    // ... plus storage, discovery, and lifecycle fields\n}\n```\n\n### 2.2 Config & Handler Chain (`pkg/server/config.go`)\n\n**Responsibility**: HTTP filter pipeline construction and request lifecycle management.\n\n**Request Flow** (constructed by `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` at line 1015):\n\n```\nRequest → AuditInit\n       → PanicRecovery\n       → MuxAndDiscoveryComplete (wait for routes ready)\n       → RequestReceivedTimestamp\n       → RequestInfo (parse path, verb, resource)\n       → WithRoutine (optional: spawn goroutine)\n       → LatencyTrackers\n       → HTTPLogging\n       → HSTS (HTTP Strict Transport Security)\n       → RetryAfter (shutdown signaling)\n       → CacheControl\n       → GoawayChance (probabilistic connection close for long polling)\n       → WatchTerminationDuringShutdown (drain watch requests on shutdown)\n       → WaitGroup (track in-flight requests)\n       → RequestDeadline (context deadline from timeout)\n       → TimeoutForNonLongRunning (apply timeout; streaming requests exempt)\n       → WarningRecorder (accumulate warning headers)\n       → CORS (Cross-Origin Resource Sharing)\n       → Authentication (identify user from cert, token, OIDC, etc.)\n       → Tracing (distributed tracing via OpenTelemetry)\n       → Audit (log request event before mutation)\n       → Impersonation (optional: act as different user)\n       → PriorityAndFairness or MaxInFlight (concurrency control)\n       → Authorization (check user permission for resource verb)\n       → REST Endpoint Handler (stored in go-restful container)\n```\n\n**Key extension point**: `Config.BuildHandlerChainFunc` allows custom servers to wrap or replace the chain entirely (line 186).\n\n### 2.3 APIServerHandler & Director (`pkg/server/handler.go`)\n\n**Responsibility**: Route requests to appropriate handler (go-restful for registered APIs or custom fallthrough).\n\n**Structure**:\n- `FullHandlerChain`: Complete HTTP handler with all filters applied\n- `GoRestfulContainer`: HTTP service container for registered REST endpoints (go-restful)\n- `NonGoRestfulMux`: Fallthrough mux for non-API paths (health checks, metrics, webhooks)\n- `Director`: Smart dispatcher determining which handler to use\n\nThe Director introspects the request path to determine if it matches a go-restful route. If yes, delegates to `GoRestfulContainer`; otherwise, delegates to `NonGoRestfulMux`. This avoids forcing all paths through the heavyweight go-restful machinery.\n\n### 2.4 APIGroupInfo & APIInstaller (`pkg/server/config.go`, `pkg/endpoints/installer.go`)\n\n**Responsibility**: Register API groups' resources as HTTP endpoints and enable discovery.\n\n**APIGroupInfo** (line 71 in config.go) bundles:\n- `PrioritizedVersions`: Ordered list of supported API versions (e.g., `[v1, v1beta1]`)\n- `VersionedResourcesStorageMap`: Nested map `{version → {resource → rest.Storage}}`\n- `Scheme`: Type registry for serialization, conversion, defaulting\n- `NegotiatedSerializer`: Codec factory for JSON, YAML, Protocol Buffers\n\n**Installation Process** (line 876 in genericapiserver.go):\n1. `InstallAPIGroups()` validates group/version presence\n2. `getOpenAPIModels()` introspects storage and types to generate OpenAPI schema\n3. `installAPIResources()` uses `APIInstaller` to create REST routes for each resource\n4. Discovery endpoints registered at `/apis/{group}/{version}`\n\n**APIInstaller** creates HTTP handlers for CRUD operations:\n- `GET /apis/apps/v1/namespaces/{namespace}/deployments/{name}` → `Get` handler\n- `POST /apis/apps/v1/namespaces/{namespace}/deployments` → `Create` handler\n- `PATCH /apis/apps/v1/namespaces/{namespace}/deployments/{name}` → `Patch` handler\n- `DELETE /apis/apps/v1/namespaces/{namespace}/deployments/{name}` → `Delete` handler\n- `WATCH /apis/apps/v1/namespaces/{namespace}/deployments` → `Watch` handler\n\n### 2.5 Authentication (`pkg/authentication/`)\n\n**Responsibility**: Identify the user and extract identity information.\n\n**Pluggable Authenticators** (union pattern):\n- `x509/client-cert`: Extract identity from client certificate CN and O fields\n- `bearer-token`: Validate JWT, service account tokens, or custom bearer tokens\n- `request-header`: Proxy authentication (X-Remote-User, X-Remote-Groups headers)\n- `oidc`: OpenID Connect token validation\n- `webhook`: Out-of-tree webhook for custom token validation\n\n**Key Property**: The *first* authenticator in the chain that successfully identifies the user wins. Others are not consulted.\n\n**User Info** includes:\n- Name, UID\n- Groups (for RBAC)\n- Extra fields (for custom attributes)\n\n### 2.6 Authorization (`pkg/authorization/`)\n\n**Responsibility**: Decide if an authenticated user is allowed to perform an action.\n\n**Pluggable Authorizers** (union pattern):\n- `rbac`: Role-Based Access Control (core authorization model)\n- `node`: Special rules for kubelet system requests\n- `webhook`: Custom authorization logic via webhook\n- `always-allow`: Unconditional allow (insecure, testing only)\n- `always-deny`: Unconditional deny\n\n**Key Property**: Authorizers vote on requests. First authorizer to **allow** → request allowed. First to **deny** → request denied. If all abstain (no opinion) → deny by default.\n\n### 2.7 Admission Control (`pkg/admission/`)\n\n**Responsibility**: Validate or mutate request objects before storage, enforce policies.\n\n**Two Phases**:\n1. **Mutating Admission Plugins**: Can modify the object (e.g., inject sidecar containers, fill defaults)\n2. **Validating Admission Plugins**: Can only reject (e.g., enforce Pod Security Standards)\n\n**Plugins**:\n- Built-in: PodSecurity, ResourceQuota, PodDisruptionBudget\n- Dynamic webhooks: ValidatingAdmissionWebhook, MutatingAdmissionWebhook\n- Dynamic policies (CEL-based): ValidatingAdmissionPolicy\n\n**Key Property**: Object deserialization happens *after* auth/authz/admission initialization but *before* validation (line 113 in ARCHITECTURE.md states \"request body is not deserialized until it has passed authentication and authorization\"). This prevents parsing attacks from expensive resources.\n\n### 2.8 Storage Interface & Registry (`pkg/registry/`, `pkg/storage/`)\n\n**Responsibility**: Abstract data persistence layer; enable mock/test implementations and multiple backends.\n\n**Core Interface** (`pkg/storage/interfaces.go`):\n- `Get(ctx, key string, opts GetOptions) (*stored.RuntimeObject, error)`\n- `Create(ctx, key string, obj, out runtime.Object, ttl *uint64) error`\n- `Delete(ctx, key string, obj runtime.Object, preconditions *Preconditions) error`\n- `Watch(ctx, key string, opts WatchOptions) (watch.Interface, error)`\n- `List(ctx, key string, opts ListOptions) (*List, error)`\n- `GuaranteedUpdate(ctx, key string, ptrToType runtime.Object, ignoreNotFound bool, preconditions *Preconditions, tryUpdate UpdateFunc) error`\n\n**Implementations**:\n- **etcd3 backend** (`pkg/storage/etcd3/`): gRPC client for etcd key-value store\n- **Memory backend** (testing): In-process map storage\n- **Disk backend** (testing): File-system key-value storage\n\n**Watch Caching** (`pkg/storage/cacher/`):\n- Maintains in-memory buffer of recent objects and events\n- Serves most List/Watch requests from cache, reducing etcd load\n- Automatically refreshes from backend when consistency required\n- Uses bookmarks to track progress for unchanged objects\n\n### 2.9 REST Storage Strategy (`pkg/registry/generic/`)\n\n**Responsibility**: Resource-specific business logic for validation, versioning, and object lifecycle.\n\n**Strategy Interface** (in rest package):\n- `New()`: Allocate empty object instance\n- `NewList()`: Allocate empty list instance\n- `NamespaceScoped()`: Declare if resource is namespaced or cluster-scoped\n- `ShortNames()`, `Synonyms()`: Aliases for discovery\n- `Validate(ctx, obj)`: Validate object invariants\n- `ValidateUpdate(ctx, objOld, objNew)`: Validate update transitions\n- `PrepareForCreate()`: Mutate object before creation (e.g., set defaults)\n- `PrepareForUpdate()`: Mutate object before update\n\n**Example**: The Deployment strategy might validate that `replicas >= 0` and reject updates that change the deployment's selector labels (immutable).\n\n### 2.10 Server Chain Delegation\n\nThe production kube-apiserver is **not a single server** but a **chain of three GenericAPIServer instances**:\n\n```\nRequest → AggregatorServer\n            ↓\n          (check APIService objects)\n          ↓ match → proxy to extension API server\n          ↓ no match → delegate to next\n          ↓\n        KubeAPIServer (core Kubernetes APIs)\n            ↓\n          (handle /api/v1, /apis/apps/v1, etc.)\n          ↓ match → serve from storage\n          ↓ no match → delegate to next\n          ↓\n        APIExtensionsServer (CustomResourceDefinition handler)\n            ↓\n          (handle /apis/apiextensions.k8s.io)\n          ↓ match → serve CRD resources\n          ↓ no match → 404 Not Found\n```\n\nEach server in the chain implements `DelegationTarget` interface (line 308 in genericapiserver.go), enabling composition without tight coupling.\n\n---\n\n## 3. End-to-End Request Flow with Code Evidence\n\n### Scenario: Client creates a Deployment via kube-apiserver\n\n**Step 1: Request Arrives at FullHandlerChain**\n- Client: `POST /apis/apps/v1/namespaces/default/deployments`\n- Handler: `APIServerHandler.FullHandlerChain` (line 45 in handler.go)\n\n**Step 2: Request Parsing & Request Info**\n- Filter: `WithRequestInfo` (endpoints/filters/requestinfo.go) parses path → extracts resource, verb, namespace\n- Result: `apirequest.RequestInfo` attached to context\n\n**Step 3: Authentication**\n- Filter: `WithAuthentication` (endpoints/filters/authentication.go)\n- Authenticator: Union of x509, bearer-token, OIDC, webhook\n- Result: `user.Info` attached to context if successful; 401 Unauthorized if fails\n\n**Step 4: Authorization**\n- Filter: `WithAuthorization` (endpoints/filters/authorization.go)\n- Authorizer: RBAC checks if user has `create` verb on `deployments` resource\n- Result: 403 Forbidden if denied; pass through if allowed\n\n**Step 5: Priority & Fairness / Max In-Flight**\n- Filter: `WithPriorityAndFairness` or `WithMaxInFlightLimit` (server/filters/)\n- Logic: Classify request by FlowSchema, assign PriorityLevel; queue if system overloaded\n- Result: Request waits in queue if needed; 429 Too Many Requests if rejected\n\n**Step 6: Admission Control (Initialization)**\n- Filter: `WithAudit` starts audit event (endpoints/filters/audit.go)\n- Object **not yet deserialized** to reduce parsing overhead\n\n**Step 7: Request Timeout & Deadline**\n- Filters: `WithTimeoutForNonLongRunning`, `WithRequestDeadline`\n- Logic: Set context deadline based on request timeout; watch requests exempt\n- Result: Request canceled if deadline exceeded\n\n**Step 8: Director Routing**\n- `APIServerHandler.Director` introspects request path\n- Decision: Matches `/apis/apps/v1` → route to `GoRestfulContainer`\n\n**Step 9: Go-Restful Routing & Handler Selection**\n- `GoRestfulContainer` finds matching web service and route\n- Route: `POST /namespaces/{namespace}/deployments` → `Create` handler (endpoints/handlers/create.go)\n\n**Step 10: Request Body Deserialization**\n- Handler: Deserializes JSON body into `*apps.Deployment` using codec from Scheme\n- Storage: Retrieves ResourceVersion from etcd to validate version field\n\n**Step 11: Admission Control (Mutation & Validation)**\n- Plugins: Mutating plugins may inject sidecars, defaulters fill optional fields\n- Plugins: Validating plugins check Pod Security Standards, resource quotas\n- Result: 400 Bad Request if validation fails\n\n**Step 12: REST Storage Create**\n- Handler calls: `storage.Create(ctx, name, obj, ...)`\n- Cacher wraps the actual etcd backend\n- Flow:\n  - Prepare object (clear metadata, assign ResourceVersion)\n  - etcd3 client: `PUT /deployments/{namespace}/{name}` with transaction\n  - Cacher invalidates cache entry\n  - Return created object with `metadata.resourceVersion` set\n\n**Step 13: Audit Logging**\n- Filter: `WithAudit` logs event with result, object, user info (audit level: Metadata, RequestResponse, etc.)\n\n**Step 14: Response Serialization**\n- Handler: Serialize created object to JSON (Content-Type: application/json)\n- Status: 201 Created with Location header\n\n**Step 15: ResponseWriter & Filters**\n- Filters: `WithLatencyTrackers`, `WithWarningRecorder`, `WithCacheControl` add response headers\n- Result: Client receives response\n\n---\n\n## 4. Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs\n\n### 4.1 Resource Version Conflicts\n\n**Scenario**: Two clients update same Deployment concurrently.\n\n**Mechanism** (optimistic locking):\n- Client A reads Deployment `resourceVersion: 12345`\n- Client B reads Deployment `resourceVersion: 12345`\n- Client B updates → etcd revision advances to 12346\n- Client A attempts update with `resourceVersion: 12345` (now stale)\n- etcd compares: stored version (12346) ≠ provided version (12345)\n- Result: `409 Conflict` returned; Client A must retry (read fresh, merge, resubmit)\n\n**Tradeoff**: Optimistic concurrency is simple and lock-free but requires clients to handle conflicts. This is acceptable because Kubernetes expects controllers to retry on 409.\n\n### 4.2 Watch Cache Staleness Bounds\n\n**Problem**: Watch cache may lag behind etcd if object unchanged but other objects changed.\n\n**Solution** (bookmarks, line 213 in ARCHITECTURE.md):\n- Cacher periodically sends bookmark events (`type: BOOKMARK`) for unchanged objects\n- Bookmark includes current ResourceVersion, signaling to clients that cache is fresh as of that version\n- Prevents expensive relist operations when cache position is old but no events for specific resource\n\n**Tradeoff**: Bookmarks add message overhead (more events) but reduce thundering-herd relist storms when watchers are many.\n\n### 4.3 Request Timeout & Long-Running Requests\n\n**Problem**: Generic timeout logic unsuitable for watches, port-forward, exec which stream indefinitely.\n\n**Solution** (line 1067 in config.go):\n- `LongRunningFunc` identifies requests by verb (watch, exec, attach, proxy)\n- Short timeout applied to CRUD operations (default 60s)\n- No timeout for identified long-running requests\n- Separate goroutine handles timeout logic; original request can keep writing response\n\n**Tradeoff**: Requires explicit classification of long-running verbs; custom servers must extend `LongRunningFunc` if adding new streaming endpoints.\n\n### 4.4 Memory Exhaustion from Large Objects\n\n**Limit**: `MaxRequestBodyBytes` (line 466 in config.go, default 3 MiB).\n\n**Mechanism**: Request body size checked before deserialization (prevents parsing large payloads).\n\n**Tradeoff**: Hardcoded limit prevents DOS but may block legitimate large bulk operations (e.g., large ConfigMap). Mitigated by allowing configuration; extensions can adjust for their workloads.\n\n### 4.5 Thundering Herd in Watch Cache\n\n**Problem**: Large resource lists with rapid updates cause all watchers to receive events, overwhelming client bandwidth.\n\n**Mitigation** (in cacher):\n- Bookmark events space out ResourceVersion updates, preventing repeated sends of same version\n- Rate limiting on event emission prevents queue explosion\n- Old events dropped from buffer once new events arrive\n\n**Tradeoff**: Watchers may miss intermediate states if buffer overflows, but most applications only care about final state anyway.\n\n### 4.6 Admission Plugin Ordering & Re-invocation\n\n**Problem**: Mutating plugins may change objects in ways that trigger validating plugin denials; order matters.\n\n**Solution** (line 34 in ARCHITECTURE.md, \"re-invocation policy\"):\n- Mutating plugins run first; validating plugins run second\n- If mutating plugin changes object, validating plugins re-run on modified object\n- Circular mutations prevented by re-invocation control (a plugin's mutations don't trigger other plugins' mutations indefinitely)\n\n**Tradeoff**: Re-invocation adds complexity and latency; plugins must declare which fields they mutate.\n\n### 4.7 Discovery & OpenAPI Latency\n\n**Problem**: Generating OpenAPI spec from all installed resources is expensive (reflection + JSON serialization).\n\n**Solution** (line 239 in ARCHITECTURE.md):\n- `openapi-gen` tool pre-generates Go functions that build spec once at startup\n- Spec cached and served statically thereafter; no runtime generation\n\n**Tradeoff**: Spec is stale if new CRD installed (solved by cache invalidation on CRD add/delete, but adds latency).\n\n---\n\n## 5. Extension Points, Customization Hooks, and Associated Risks\n\n### 5.1 Custom Admission Plugins\n\n**Extension Point**: `admission.Interface` (pkg/admission/interfaces.go)\n\n**How**:\n1. Implement `Admit(ctx context.Context, attrs Attributes, o ObjectInterfaces) error`\n2. Inspect attributes (user, resource, object)\n3. Mutate object (mutating) or return error (validating)\n4. Register plugin via `admission.Registry`\n\n**Example Use Case**: Custom sidecar injector for security policies.\n\n**Risks**:\n- **Latency**: Plugin runs on every write; slow plugins block all mutations\n- **Denial of Service**: Plugin can unconditionally reject all writes\n- **Object Leakage**: Plugin receives full object including secrets; must not log confidential fields\n- **Cascading Failures**: If plugin crashes, admission control chain halts; other plugins not consulted\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Webhooks (dynamic, can be disabled without restart) preferred over compiled plugins\n- Timeout per plugin (if supported) prevents indefinite hangs\n- Audit plugins' object access to detect data leaks\n\n### 5.2 Custom Authorization Plugins\n\n**Extension Point**: `authorizer.Authorizer` (pkg/authorization/authorizer/interfaces.go)\n\n**How**:\n1. Implement `Authorize(ctx context.Context, a Attributes) (Decision, Reason, Error)`\n2. Return `Allow`, `Deny`, or `NoOpinion`\n3. Register via `authorizer.NewUnion(...)`\n\n**Example Use Case**: Custom ABAC (Attribute-Based Access Control) for dynamic policies.\n\n**Risks**:\n- **Privilege Escalation**: Incorrect policy grants unintended access\n- **Latency**: Authorization check on every request; slow authorizers block all operations\n- **Policy Inconsistency**: Mismatch between local authorization and webhook authorization\n- **Cascading Denial**: If all authorizers abstain, request denied; client can't determine why\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Test authorization rules against audit logs (see what decisions were made)\n- Webhook authorizers allow disabling without restart\n- Explicit deny policies prevent accidental privilege escalation\n\n### 5.3 Custom Storage Backends\n\n**Extension Point**: `rest.Storage` interface (pkg/registry/rest/)\n\n**How**:\n1. Implement CRUD operations: `Create()`, `Get()`, `Update()`, `Delete()`, `List()`, `Watch()`\n2. Pass implementation in `APIGroupInfo.VersionedResourcesStorageMap`\n3. `InstallAPIGroup()` registers the storage as HTTP endpoint handler\n\n**Example Use Case**: Custom database (PostgreSQL, Cassandra) instead of etcd.\n\n**Risks**:\n- **Watch Semantics Deviation**: Custom backends must respect Kubernetes watch semantics (incremental events, resource versions) or clients fail\n- **Consistency Violations**: If custom backend doesn't support transactions, updates may partially succeed\n- **ResourceVersion Mapping**: Custom backend must map internal versions to Kubernetes resource versions; mismatch breaks optimistic locking\n- **List Continuation**: Pagination via `continue` token requires backend understanding of list semantics\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Use `genericregistry.Store` wrapper which handles versioning and watch semantics\n- Test custom storage against Kubernetes client conformance tests\n\n### 5.4 Custom Handler Chain\n\n**Extension Point**: `Config.BuildHandlerChainFunc` (line 186 in config.go)\n\n**How**:\n```go\nconfig.BuildHandlerChainFunc = func(apiHandler http.Handler, c *Config) http.Handler {\n    handler := apiHandler\n    handler = myCustomFilter(handler)  // Add custom filter\n    handler = DefaultBuildHandlerChain(handler, c)  // Wrap with standard chain\n    return handler\n}\n```\n\n**Example Use Case**: Custom audit filter, request mutation, or telemetry.\n\n**Risks**:\n- **Filter Order Violation**: Custom filter placed incorrectly may see unauthenticated requests or unresolved resource names\n- **Context Mutation**: If custom filter modifies request context, downstream handlers see corrupted state\n- **Panic Recovery**: If custom filter panics, bypasses panic recovery if placed after `WithPanicRecovery`\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Document filter ordering expectations in comments\n- Use standard `http.Handler` middleware patterns (defer cleanup, check context)\n- Test with malformed requests to ensure robustness\n\n### 5.5 PostStartHooks & PreShutdownHooks\n\n**Extension Point**: `GenericAPIServer.AddPostStartHook()` (genericapiserver.go)\n\n**How**:\n```go\nserver.AddPostStartHook(\"my-initializer\", func(ctx PostStartHookContext) error {\n    // Initialize custom components after server starts listening\n    return nil\n})\n```\n\n**Lifecycle**:\n- `PostStartHooks` run sequentially after server begins listening on secure port\n- `PreShutdownHooks` run sequentially when server receives shutdown signal (SIGTERM)\n- If hook returns error, server panics (halts startup/shutdown)\n\n**Example Use Cases**: Initialize custom controllers, health checks, cleanup connections.\n\n**Risks**:\n- **Deadlock**: Hook waits for resource that requires server (e.g., listing Deployments); creates circular dependency\n- **Blocking Startup**: Long-running hook delays server readiness; clients fail with connection refused\n- **Lost State**: If hook fails halfway, partial initialization leaves inconsistent state\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Use timeout for hooks (context includes deadline)\n- Return errors immediately without waiting for resources\n- Idempotent cleanup in PreShutdownHooks (may be called multiple times)\n\n### 5.6 REST Routes & Subroutes\n\n**Extension Point**: `GenericAPIServer.NonGoRestfulMux` (handler.go, line 51)\n\n**How**:\n```go\nserver.Handler.NonGoRestfulMux.HandleFunc(\"/custom/path\", http.HandlerFunc(func(w http.ResponseWriter, r *http.Request) {\n    // Custom handler\n}))\n```\n\n**Example Use Case**: Webhooks, health checks, debug endpoints.\n\n**Risks**:\n- **Path Collision**: Custom path may shadow built-in paths (e.g., /healthz)\n- **Missing Auth**: Custom handlers not protected by authentication/authorization filters\n- **Inconsistent Response**: Custom handlers may not respect content negotiation, error formatting\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Use unique paths to avoid collisions (e.g., `/custom/v1/...`)\n- Manually apply auth filters if sensitive\n- Document custom endpoint contracts for clients\n\n---\n\n## 6. Operational Implications\n\n### 6.1 Request Timeout Tuning\n\n**Default**: 60 seconds for non-long-running requests (line 445 in config.go).\n\n**Operational Concern**: Timeout applies to entire request lifecycle including:\n- Authentication check (may call webhooks)\n- Authorization check (may call webhooks)\n- Admission control (may call webhooks, mutation plugins)\n- Storage I/O (etcd latency)\n\n**Long Webhooks Impact**: If auth webhook takes 30s, only 30s remains for remaining stages; admission webhook fails with timeout.\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Monitor webhook latencies (metrics: `apiserver_authorization_decision_latency`, `apiserver_admission_webhook_latency`)\n- Increase timeout if webhooks are slow, but accept increased user latency\n- Implement webhook caching to reduce roundtrips\n\n### 6.2 Watch Cache Sizing\n\n**Default**: In-memory cache per resource, size not explicitly limited by apiserver (depends on object count and size).\n\n**Operational Concern**: Large clusters with many resources (e.g., 1M Pods) can cause memory exhaustion. Watch cache holds all objects in memory.\n\n**Example**: 1M Pods × 1 KiB per Pod = ~1 GiB memory; with overhead, may reach 2-3 GiB.\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Monitor memory usage (metric: `apiserver_watch_cache_capacity`)\n- Reduce watch cache scope (disable for large resources via storage config)\n- Use etcd backend's watch streams directly (bypass cache) if memory critical\n\n### 6.3 Admission Webhook Cascading Failures\n\n**Scenario**: Mutating webhook fails (e.g., sidecar injector crashes).\n\n**Impact**: All Pod creations fail with 500 Internal Server Error (if webhook deadline exceeded) or 403 Forbidden (if webhook returns deny).\n\n**Operational Concern**: **Cascading failure**: If webhook is misconfigured, entire cluster becomes unable to schedule new Pods.\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Webhook failurePolicy: `Fail` (deny on failure, safe) vs `Ignore` (allow on failure, unsafe)\n- Set webhook timeout low (e.g., 5s) so cascading failures are transient\n- Monitor webhook response times; alert if 95th percentile latency grows\n- Disable webhook if necessary: `kubectl delete validatingwebhookconfigurations <name>`\n\n### 6.4 Audit Logging Volume\n\n**Default**: Audit policy controls verbosity (line 249 in ARCHITECTURE.md).\n\n**Operational Concern**: High audit level (`RequestResponse`) logs full request/response bodies; can generate 1 GiB/hour in busy clusters.\n\n**Example**: Each request logged includes entire object, which for bulk operations (apply 1000 objects) multiplies storage.\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Use audit level `Metadata` (log metadata only, not bodies) for most requests\n- Use `RequestResponse` for sensitive resources (Secrets, ServiceAccounts) only\n- Configure log rotation/retention (e.g., 30 days)\n- Stream audit logs to centralized system (SIEM) for retention compliance\n\n### 6.5 Concurrency Limits & Pressure Shedding\n\n**Default**: `MaxRequestsInFlight: 400`, `MaxMutatingRequestsInFlight: 200` (line 443-444 in config.go).\n\n**Behavior**: Requests exceeding limits are queued (if Priority & Fairness enabled) or rejected with 429 Too Many Requests.\n\n**Operational Concern**: If limit is too low, legitimate traffic rejected. If too high, server memory exhausted by buffered requests.\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Monitor request rates (metric: `apiserver_request_total`)\n- Adjust limits based on cluster size and workload (e.g., larger clusters need higher limits)\n- Implement client-side retry backoff (exponential with jitter) to avoid thundering herd on 429\n\n### 6.6 Discovery & OpenAPI Serving Latency\n\n**Default**: OpenAPI spec served from static cache (line 243 in ARCHITECTURE.md).\n\n**Operational Concern**: First client to call `/openapi/v2` triggers spec generation (if not cached), causing latency spike.\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Pre-warm cache during server startup by explicitly building spec\n- Monitor endpoint latency (metric: `apiserver_request_duration_seconds` with endpoint=/openapi/v2)\n- Cache spec response in client-side libraries (client-go does this)\n\n---\n\n## 7. Important Files and Directories Reference\n\n### Core Server\n- `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/genericapiserver.go`: GenericAPIServer struct, API group installation\n- `staging/src/k8s.io/apiextensions-apiserver/`: CustomResourceDefinition handler\n\n---\n\n## 8. Design Principles & Philosophy\n\n### Layered Extensibility\n\nThe apiserver library is designed as **concentric rings**:\n\n1. **Core**: `GenericAPIServer` (non-negotiable infrastructure)\n2. **Filter Chain**: Pluggable filters (authentication, authorization, admission)\n3. **Storage**: Abstract backend (etcd, custom implementations)\n4. **Handlers**: CRUD operation handlers (can be overridden)\n5. **Routes**: Custom HTTP routes attached to `NonGoRestfulMux`\n\nThis layering allows extensions at any level without forcing recompilation of the entire server.\n\n### Separation of Concerns\n\n- **Authentication** (who?) is separate from **authorization** (what?), which is separate from **admission** (should we?).\n- Storage interface decoupled from HTTP layer, enabling alternative backends.\n- Admission plugins isolated from core CRUD logic, allowing dynamic enable/disable.\n\n### Security by Default\n\n- Request body deserialized *after* auth/authz to prevent parsing attacks.\n- Admission control is *required*; server cannot run with empty chain (defaults to allow-all, but still a chain).\n- Authorization defaults to deny; must be explicitly allowed.\n\n### Observability\n\n- Handler chain instrumented with latency trackers, metrics, and audit logging.\n- Every filter stage tracked (metric: `apiserver_request_duration_seconds` with `le` bucket and `endpoint` label).\n- Audit trail maintained for compliance and debugging.\n\n---\n\n## 9. Conclusion\n\nThe Kubernetes apiserver library abstracts away the plumbing required to build REST APIs that behave like Kubernetes. By providing reusable components for authentication, authorization, admission, storage, and request handling, it enables:\n\n1. **Consistency**: Custom API servers behave like official kube-apiserver (same patterns, same mechanisms)\n2. **Extensibility**: Plugins for admission control, authorization, custom storage\n3. **Reliability**: Built-in rate limiting, timeout handling, graceful shutdown\n4. **Observability**: Metrics, audit logging, distributed tracing integration\n\nExtensions should be carefully tested, as they run in the critical path of every API request. Production deployments should monitor webhook latencies, admission decision rates, and authorization errors to detect cascading failures early.\n\nThe architectural patterns documented in `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/ARCHITECTURE.md` and the reference implementation at `staging/src/k8s.io/sample-apiserver/` serve as guides for building compatible extension servers.\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":9,"cache_creation_input_tokens":905,"cache_read_input_tokens":81214,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":905},"output_tokens":1,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"3681fddd-bd37-4a65-b86f-7ae594ec71f5","uuid":"a665e983-e04d-4092-b522-9ef26a6993c9"}
